      E XPLORING N EW F RONTIERS IN V ERTICAL F EDER -
002   ATED L EARNING : THE ROLE OF S ADDLE P OINT R E -

011                                               A BSTRACT
012
013            The objective of Vertical Federated Learning (VFL) is to collectively train a model
014            using features available on different devices while sharing the same users. This pa-
015            per focuses on the saddle point reformulation of the VFL problem via the classical
016
               Lagrangian function. We first demonstrate how this formulation can be solved us-
               ing deterministic methods. More importantly, we explore various stochastic mod-
017
               ifications to adapt to practical scenarios, such as employing compression tech-
018
               niques for efficient information transmission, enabling partial participation for
019            asynchronous communication, and utilizing coordinate selection for faster local
020            computation. We show that the saddle point reformulation plays a key role and
021            opens up possibilities to use mentioned extension that seem to be impossible in the
022            standard minimization formulation. Convergence estimates are provided for each
023            algorithm, demonstrating their effectiveness in addressing the VFL problem. Ad-
024            ditionally, alternative reformulations are investigated, and numerical experiments
025            are conducted to validate performance and effectiveness of the proposed approach.
026
027
028   1   I NTRODUCTION
029
030   Federated Learning is an emergent paradigm that involves training a model on private data from
031   several devices. It can be divided into two types: horizontal (HFL)(Konečnỳ et al., 2016; McMahan
032   et al., 2017), where data samples are distributed across clients, and vertical (VFL) (Liu et al., 2022;
033
      Yang et al., 2023; Wei et al., 2022; Khan et al., 2023) with orthogonal data partitioning. In contrast
      to HFL, VFL divides the features of the same samples across clients. In this paper, we focus on
034
      the VFL problem, which appears in various fields from scoring problems (Chen et al., 2021a) to
035
      healthcare (Dankar et al., 2019) and smart manufacturing (Ge et al., 2021).
036
037   Since we deal with a distributed environment in both horizontal and vertical data partitioning, the
038   organization of the communication process plays a crucial role in developing learning algorithms.
039
      Due to the difference in the formulations, unique characteristics and issues can arise. The HFL
      problem statement is very similar to the classical distributed cluster learning (Verbraeken et al.,
040
      2020), therefore, the study of various kinds of specialized HFL algorithms that take into account
041
      different aspects ranging from communication efficiency to personalization is quite extensive and
042   comprehensive (Kairouz et al., 2021). It is a natural idea to transfer most of the techniques and
043   useful stories from the horizontal scenario to the vertical one. And there are such results – see e.g.
044   (Liu et al., 2022, Table 3), but not many at the moment. This can be due to the fact that the VFL
045   problem is more ambiguous and complex from a formal optimization point of view, then it is not
046   easy to use the theory from HFL.
047
      Formally, VFL can be viewed as a classical minimization problem, with specifics in calculating the
048   loss function, its gradient, or possibly higher-order derivatives. But there is another way to look at.
049   In particular, the VFL problem can be rewritten as an augmented Lagrangian (Boyd et al., 2011,
050   Section 8), which can be solved using the ADMM method (Glowinski & Marroco, 1975; Gabay &
051   Mercier, 1976). Recent works argue that such a view of VFL is more private (Hu et al., 2019; Xie
052   et al., 2022b). The augmented Lagrangian reformulation combined with the ADMM algorithm is a
053   powerful tool for solving many practical optimization problems (not just VFL) (Bioucas-Dias &
      Figueiredo, 2010; Forero et al., 2010; Wahlberg et al., 2012; Wang & Banerjee, 2013; Sedghi et al.,


                                                        1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      2014; Li et al., 2014). It provides privacy and an efficient solution for various scenarios, offering
055   superior performance compared to other methods, making it a viable choice.
056
057   In spite of this, the already mentioned modifications from (Liu et al., 2022, Table 3) focus on the
058
      basic minimization formulation. Notably, many of these results are often empirical and lack a theo-
      retical foundation for convergence. Moreover, current results around the Lagrangian statement have
059
      also shortcomings and are weakly studied. In particular, the widely-used approach to VFL based
060
      on ADMM is costly, as two additional minimization subproblems must be solved on each iteration.
061   Thus, we propose to expand the theory around saddle point reformulations in this paper since the
062   augmented Lagrangian reformulation is a good alternative to the classical minimization formulation.
063   In particular, we address three research questions:
064
            1. Is there any other way to rewrite the VFL problem which can provide advantages to the
065
               standard minimization formulation?
066
            2. What basic method should be used to solve the new VFL problem reformulation?
067         3. Is it possible to modify the basic method for practical use?
068
069
      1.1   O UR CONTRIBUTION
070
071   • New look at VFL. We first consider the VFL reformulation via classical Lagrangian and show that
072   if the original VFL problem is convex, then the reformulation is convex-concave Saddle Point Prob-
073   lem (SPP), hence methods for SPP, such as ExtraGradient (Korpelevich, 1977; Nemirovski,
074   2004), can be applied to it.
075
      • New basic method for VFL. The classical Lagrangian is a convex-concave SPP that can be solved
076
      using optimal methods. We introduce the basic deterministic algorithm and its efficient stochastic
077   modifications for VFL and prove that they significantly outperform existing techniques, e.g., ADMM,
078   in terms of iteration cost (Table 1 in Appendix A).
079
080
      • Family of practical modifications. We present various modifications of the basic version of
      the algorithm to address practical needs and to make the basic algorithm more robust, including i)
081
      introducing compression operators to reduce the amount of transmitted information and solve the
082
      communication bottleneck (Alistarh et al., 2017; 2018); ii) allowing partial participation for asyn-
083   chronous device communication (Ribero & Vikalo, 2020); iii) a coordinate modification to reduce
084   the cost of local computing (Nesterov, 2012). Moreover, we show that the saddle reformulation
085   allows to fully reveal the possibilities of these modifications.
086
      • More VFL reformulations. We consider additional saddle point reformulations of the VFL prob-
087
      lem, which have advantages, such as easier stepsize estimation, but require extra memory or exis-
088
      tence of dual function of the loss.
089
090   • Extension to non-convex problems. We show how our approach can be generalized to handle
091   non-convex learning problems. It is worth noting that all modifications are easily transferable.
092   • Numerical experiments. We show empirically that our approach can outperform existing VFL
093   solutions in the standard minimization formulation and the saddle problem reformulation.
094
095   1.2   T ECHNICAL PRELIMINARIES

      We also need two classical definitions for the function f .
103
104   Definition 1.1. The function f : Rd → R, is L-smooth, if there exists a constant L > 0 such that
105   ∀x, y ∈ Rd ∥∇f (x) − ∇f (y)∥ ≤ L∥x − y∥.
106   Definition 1.2. The function f : Rd → R, is convex, if f (x) ≥ f (y) + ⟨∇f (y), x − y⟩ for all
107   x, y ∈ Rd .

      2    S ADDLE P OINT R EFORMULATION AND E XTRAGRADIENT
109
110
      Reformulation. The most common problem in machine learning, known as empirical risk mini-
111   mization (Shalev-Shwartz & Ben-David, 2014), can be formulated as follows:
112
113                                  minx∈Rd [f (x) := ℓ (Ax, b) + r(x)] ,                                  (1)
114   where x is a vector of model parameters, A ∈ Rs×d is a data matrix, b ∈ Rs is a vector of labels,
115   ℓ : Rs × Rs → R is a loss function, r : Rd → R is a separable regularizer, s is a number of
116   data samples and d is a size of the model. This paper considers a VFL setting where data is stored
117   across n different devices. Here, the matrix A is divided by columns, and each device gets different
118   features of each of the s data points (for simplicity, we assume that the matrix A contains no missing
119   data). Thus, we can rewrite (1) in the form of the VFL problem (Liu et al., 2024):
                                                 Pn                Pn
120                                minx∈Rd [ℓ ( i=1 Ai xi , b) + i=1 ri (xi )] ,                         (2)
121
122   where Ai ∈ Rs×di is a local data matrix on the i-th device, xi is a part of the parameters correspond-
123
      ing to the features of i-th device. It is natural to assume that xi lies on ith device. We additionally
      assume that labels b contain private information and are stored in the first device. Problem (2) can
124
      be rewritten as a constrained problem with additional variable z ∈ Rs :
125
                                                        Pn                  Pn
126                    minx∈Rd minz∈Rs [ℓ (z, b) + i=1 ri (xi )] , s.t.        i=1 Ai xi = z.             (3)
127
      In turn, the problem with constraints can be rewritten as a saddle point problem, where the target
128
      function is the Lagrangian function
129
                                                            Pn                  Pn
130         min(x,z)∈Rd+s maxy∈Rs L(x, z, y) := ℓ (z, b)+ i=1 ri (xi ) + y T ( i=1 Ai xi − z) . (4)
131
      Formulation (4) is the focus of our paper. Meanwhile, as we mentioned earlier, approaches    to
132                                                                                     Pn
      VFL based on ADMM also consider the Lagrangian functions with a regularizer (ρ/2)∥ i=1 Ai xi −
133
      z∥2 (we consider this case in Appendix C.1). For both reformulations, we propose a method that
134
      guarantees its convergence.
135
136   Why saddle point? Let us try to motivate the use of the saddle point reformulation (4) instead of
137   the classical minimization problem (1) with the following example.
138   If we consider the classical formulation (1), which is valid for both vertical and horizontal cases, the
139   main difference between these two types of data partitioning is the nature of the gradient computa-
140   tion, in particular concerning the communication process. In the horizontal case of (1), all workers
                                                                                       Pn
141   have the same parameter vectors but different training samples: ℓ(Ax, b) = i=1 ℓ(Âi x, bi ), where
142   Âi ∈ Rsi ×d , bi ∈ Rsi . To compute the gradient, we simply accumulate ∇x ℓ(Âj xk , b̂j ) from all
143                              Pn               k
      the workers: ∇f (x) =         j=1 ∇x ℓ(Âj x , b̂j ). In the vertical case, to calculate the gradient for
144   the parameters xi stored on the ithPdevice, it is necessary to obtain Aj xj from all the devices:
145                                           n
      ∇xi f (x) = ATi ∇z ℓ(z, b) with z = j=1 Aj xj .
146
147   In modern applications, various kinds of stochasticity arise in communication: compression to speed
148   up information transfer or random noise for privacy (Abadi et al., 2016). Let us consider the simplest
149   model in which the stochasticity of communication is additive to the package on which it acts:
150
      package + noise ξ. As we discussed, we send different things in the horizontal and vertical cases.
      More specifically, the randomness we introduce has the following effect on the true gradients:
151
                  Pn                 k          ˆ      Pn             k
                                                                                 Pn ˆ
152   ∇f (x) →        j=1 [∇x ℓ(Âj x , b̂j ) + ξj ] =  j=1 ∇x ℓ(Âj x , b̂j ) +  j=1 ξj in the horizontal case,
153                                                              n
               ∇xi f (x) → ATi ∇z ℓ(z, b), where z = j=1 Aj xj + ξj , in the vertical one.
                                                             P
154
155   A key detail can be seen here: the simplest additive stochasticity in the horizontal case remains
156   additive, but in the vertical case, the influence of randomness dips much more firmly into the gradient
157   structure. Let us look at how this kind of stochasticity affects the saddle point reformulation (4).
158   One can note that  Pitn is also necessary to collect Aj xj during gradient computing. InPmore
                                                                                                n
                                                                                                      details,
159   ∇y L(x, z, y) = j=1 Aj xj − z. With communication stochasticity this transfers to j=1 [Aj xj +
                Pn               Pn
160   ξj ]−z = j=1 Aj xj + j=1 ξj −z. The impact of randomness is additive. Because the saddle point
161   reformulation ”separates” the loss function ℓ and the data matrix A, the influence of stochasticity
      becomes more straightforward compared to (1).

162
      Before moving to stochastic methods, we must learn a deterministic algorithm as a base for con-
163   structing.
164
165   Basic method. The most straightforward idea is to solve the saddle problem using the gradient
166
      descent-ascent method: xk+1 = xk − γ∇x L(xk , y k ), y k+1 = y k + γ∇y L(xk , y k ). Gradient
      descent-ascent is not the best solution (4). Indeed, it gives relatively poor convergence guaran-
167
      tees for strongly convex – strongly concave problems (Browder, 1966; Rockafellar, 1969; Sibony,
168
      1970), and may diverge for convex-concave problems altogether (Goodfellow, 2016, Sections 7.2
169   and 8.2). Therefore, it is suggested to take the ExtraGradient/Mirror Prox method (Ko-
170   rpelevich, 1977; Nemirovski, 2004). The essence of this method is the use of an additional ex-
171   trapolation step: xk+1/2 = xk − γ∇x L(xk , y k ), xk+1 = xk − γ∇x L(xk+1/2 , y k+1/2 ) (the same
172   for y). It can be explained by the simplest example of a two-dimensional saddle point problem
173   minx∈R maxy∈R g(x, y) = xy.
174

205   It is important that in the formulation of Theorem 2.2 and in the definition (5), we use some bounded
206   sets X , Z, Y although the original problem (1) is unbounded. Such an assumption is standard for
207
      the analysis of methods for convex-concave problems. Criterion (5) can also be used for uncon-
      strained/unbounded problems. To do this, one can use the trick from (Nesterov, 2007) and introduce
208
      bounded sets X , Z, Y artificially as compact subsets of Rd , Rs , Rs . This trick is valid if some
209
      solution x∗ , y ∗ , z ∗ lies in X , Z, Y. Moreover, following (Beck, 2017, Theorems 3.59, 3.60), one
210   can show that in Theorem 2.2 we can use the criterion: ℓ(z̄ K , b) − ℓ(z ∗ , b) + ∥Ax̄K − z̄ K ∥, in-
211   stead of (5). It is more natural and means that Ax̄K → z̄ K and ℓ(z̄ K , b) → ℓ(Ax∗ , b), which is
212   what is required in the original problem (1) (see Section D.11 for more details). One can find a
213   simplified version of gap (Xu, 2017). If we assume the existence of some solution (x∗ , z ∗ , y ∗ ),
214   it can be used as follows: gap∗ (x, z, y) = L(x, z, y ∗ ) − L(x∗ , z ∗ , y). Theorem 2.2 can be
215   rewritten with gap∗ (x, z, y): there is no maximum in the right-hand side of the estimate, simply
      ∥x0 − x∗ ∥2 + ∥z 0 − z ∗ ∥2 + ∥y 0 − y ∗ ∥2 . But still, the use of (5) is preferable, e.g., for the already

237   but now let us note that Algorithm 1 presents several drawbacks. One notable limitation is its de-
238   terministic nature. In the subsequent section, we underscore the disadvantages of this characteristic
239   and suggest alterations to enhance the foundational version of our general approach. Another signif-
240   icant drawback of Algorithm 1 is its reliance on the knowledge of λmax (AT A). Given that parts of
241   matrix A are dispersed across different devices, determining λmax (AT A) is challenging. However,

243   contemplate a reformulation that negates the need for λmax (AT A) entirely, as discussed in Section
244
      4. Furthermore, incorporating augmentation, as outlined in (Boyd et al., 2011), can be beneficial
      and straightforward for implementation. It is crucial to highlight that any variations derived from
245
      Section 4 and Appendix C, as well as any adaptations of Algorithm 1 from Section 3, can be seam-
246
      lessly integrated. Another limitation is that Algorithm 1 assumes we can calculate the gradient of
247   the function ℓ and the function r. But not all functions even allow this. For example, one can choose
248   the ℓ1 regularizer as the function r. Or if we want to solve the constrained version of (1), we can
249   take r as an indicator function of some set X . We consider the case where ℓ and r are generally
250   non-smooth but simple in Appendix B.1.

252
      3     FAMILY OF M ODIFICATIONS
253
254
      This section presents the different modifications of Algorithm 1. These stochastic modifications
255
      are one of the main reasons for using saddle point reformulation. In any distributed optimization,
256   including federated learning, both in its vertical and horizontal setting, the issue of communication
257   organization is crucial. In particular, a lot of research is related to the efficiency to spend less time
258   on communications (Konečný et al., 2016; Smith et al., 2018; Ghosh et al., 2020; Gorbunov et al.,
259   2021), since they are from some point of view a waste of time (Kairouz et al., 2021).
260
261
      3.1 M ODIFICATION WITH QUANTIZATION FOR EFFECTIVE COMMUNICATIONS
262   Let us take a look at one of one of the main techniques in the fight for communication efficiency
263   – compression (Seide et al., 2014; Alistarh et al., 2017). The following definition can formally
264   describe the compression of communicated vectors.
265   Definition 3.1. Operator Q : Rd → Rd is called unbiased compressor/quantization if there exists a
266   constant ω ≥ 1 such that for all x ∈ Rd it holds E[Q(x)] = x, E[∥Q(x)∥2 ] ≤ ω∥x∥2 .
267
      Operator Q can be e.g., random coordinate choice or randomized rounding (Beznosikov et al., 2020).
268
269   Methods with compression in horizontal distributed learning are studied for quite a long time (Seide
      et al., 2014; Alistarh et al., 2017). Variance reduction methods provide a breakthrough here, initially


                                                           5
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


270
      proposed to solve non-distributed stochastic finite-sum problems (Schmidt et al., 2017; Defazio
271   et al., 2014; Johnson & Zhang, 2013; Nguyen et al., 2017). Several papers have shown that the
272   variance reduction technique can be transferred to the distributed case, where stochasticity appears
273   not from the random choice of the batch number but from the compression (Mishchenko et al.,
274   2019; Gorbunov et al., 2021; Qian et al., 2020). For our algorithm with unbiased compression
275   (Algorithm 2), we take the variance reduction method for saddle point problems from (Alacaoglu
276   & Malitsky, 2021). We introduce an additional sequence of points wik (reference points for xki ) and
277   uk (reference points for y k ). In contrast to the classical variance reduction technique, we do not
278   introduce reference points for the z variables since we do not communicate them. To update all
279   wik and uk synchronously, we need to generate bk ∈ {0, 1}, one can set the same random seed for
280   generating bk on all devices to avoid additional communication. Next, we have to send full vectors
281
      Awik and uk to the first device and all the others, respectively. The key is that the reference points are
282
      updated rarely, namely with low probability p, only when bk = 1 (see lines 11 – 19). When wik and
                                                                                                k+1/2
283   uk are not updated, we only send compressed vectors Q(y k+1/2 − uk ) and Q(Ai xi                − Ai wik )
284   (lines 6, 7). Sending compressed information, rarely forwarding full packages, is the main point of
285
      Algorithm 2. Theorem 3.2 gives the convergence; its proof can be found in Appendix D.3.

328   3.2   M ODIFICATION WITH BIASED COMPRESSION FOR MORE EFFECTIVE COMMUNICATIONS
329   Using unbiased compression operators is more straightforward in theory, but the most popular com-
330   pression operators in practice are biased (deterministic rounding (Horvath et al., 2019), greedy co-
331   ordinate selection (Alistarh et al., 2018), vector decomposition (Vogels et al., 2019)) and can be
332   described as follows.
333   Definition 3.3. Operator C : Rd → Rd (possibly randomized) is called a biased compressor if there
      exists a constant δ ≥ 1 such that for all x ∈ Rd it holds E∥C(x) − x∥2 ≤ 1 − 1δ ∥x∥2 .

334
335
      Using biased compressors is a complex issue. It can cause divergence even for quadratic problems
336
      (Beznosikov et al., 2020). To fix this, an error compensation technique (Stich et al., 2018; Karim-
337
      ireddy et al., 2019; Stich & Karimireddy, 2019) can be applied. This approach accumulates non-
338   transmitted information ({ek }, {eki }) and adds it to a new package at the next iteration of Algorithm
339   2.
340
       6: 1st sends C(y k+1/2 − uk + ek ) to other devices
341
          1st updates: ek+1 = y k+1/2 − uk + ek − C(y k+1/2 − uk + ek )

                                     k+1/2                         k+1/2
344       All update: ek+1
                        i   = Ai x i       − Ai wik + eki − C(Ai xi       − Ai wik + eki )
                        k+1        k              k        T     k+1/2                               k+1/2
345    8: All update: xi    = τ xi + (1 − τ )wi − γ(Ai [C(y             − uk + ek ) + uk ] + ∇ri (xi        ))

349   and proof can be found in Appendix D.4. Note that the proof techniques of Theorems 3.2 and
350   Theorem 3.4 differ considerably, just as the proofs of convergence of distributed GD with unbiased
351
      and biased compression (Mishchenko et al., 2019; Stich & Karimireddy, 2019).
352   Theorem 3.4. Let Assumption 2.1 holds. Let problem (4) be solved by Algorithm 3 (Ap-
353   pendix A) with operators q and C, which satisfy Definitionq3.3. Then for τ = 1 − p and

358   where x̄K , z̄ K , ȳ K , D2 are defined in Theorem 2.2.
359
360   The choice of optimal p is the same as Algorithm 2. It is enough to take p = β −1 , where β is the
361   compression power of C. The estimate from Theorem 3.4 shows the central theoretical problem
362   with biased compressors. If δ ∼ w, the results in Theorem 3.4 are worse than in Theorem 3.2.
363   Unfortunately, this kind of problem is inherent in all work around biased compressions – one cannot
364
      fully theoretically justify that biased compressors perform better (Gorbunov et al., 2021; Stich &
      Karimireddy, 2019; Richtárik et al., 2021). The only thing we can fight for is more or less acceptable
365
      convergence. Meanwhile, intuition and practical results show that biased operators are superior to
366
      unbiased ones (Beznosikov et al., 2020; Richtárik et al., 2021).

391   Using the same reasonings as after Theorem 3.2, one can find the optimal choice of p. In Algorithm

394   optimal choice of p is β −1 .

      3.4 C OORDINATE MODIFICATION FOR LOW- COST LOCAL COMPUTING
396
397
      The last modification is related to cheapening the cost of local computation in Algorithm 1. The
      most expensive local operations are matrix vector multiplications: Ai xki and ATi y k . To make them
398
      cheaper, we can apply to the idea of coordinate descent (Nesterov, 2012; Nesterov & Stich, 2017;
399
      Richtárik & Takáč, 2013; Qu & Richtárik, 2016) and compute not all coordinates for the resulting
400   vectors Ai xki and ATi y k but only 1, then instead of multiplying matrix by vector, we just compute
401   the scalar product of two vectors. This is implemented in the following modification of Algorithm 2.
402   The full version of algorithm (Algorithm 5) is given in Appendix A, Theorem 3.6 gives the conver-
403   gence, proof can be found in Appendix D.6.

446   Other reformulations are presented in Appendix C. Although this paper focuses primarily on the
447   classical Lagrangian, we also consider the augmented version, present an algorithm for it, and prove
448   convergence estimates. The convergence estimates of the method for the augmented Lagrangian are
449   no better (or even worse if the augmentation parameter is high) than those of the method for the clas-
450
      sical Lagrangian. That is why we focus on the non-augmented formulation and put the augmented
      one in Appendix C.1. It is important to emphasize that for all reformulations, all modifications from
451
      Section 3 can be made.

471   6    E XPERIMENTS
472
473   Regression.           We conduct experiments on the linear regression problem:
474
      minx∈Rd f (x) = 21 ∥Ax − b∥2 + λ∥x∥22 . Here, the smoothness constant of gradients is
475   L = λmax (AAT ) + λ with λ = λmax (AAT )/103 . Other smoothness constants, which we
476
      use in theory for our method, are Lℓ = 1, Lr = λ. We take mushrooms, a9a, w8a and MNIST
      datasets from LibSVM library (Chang & Lin, 2011). We vertically (by features) uniformly divide
477
      the dataset between 5 devices.
478
479   This experiment uses different formulations to compare deterministic methods for solving the VFL
480   problem. Here, we’re not focusing on the distributed nature of the problems; instead, we aim to
481   show that the saddle point reformulation using the classical Lagrangian function has merit (we in-
482
      vestigate modifications in Appendix E) and methods for solving it can compete effectively with
      other approaches.
483
484   Since there are two formulations of VFL, classical minimization and saddle point, we choose several
485   methods for each formulation. For the minimization formulation, we take GD as the most popular
      method, and AGD (Nesterov, 2003) as the theoretically unimprovable first-order method for smooth


                                                                9
          Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


486
          convex problems. For the saddle point formulation, we consider ADMM and Algorithm 1. The
487       methods are tuned according to the corresponding theory. For GD we choose step as L1 (Polyak,
488                                                                                                                                              √     √
          2020), for Nesterov – step as L1 and momentum as √L−√λ (Nesterov, 2003), for ADMM we take
489                                                         L+ λ
490       regularizer parameter equal to √ 1 T (Lu & Yang, 2023). Algorithm 1 is tuned according to
                                                                                                 λmax (AA )
491       Theorem 2.2 with and without β-trick (see disscusion after Corollary 2.3). The application of the
492       β-trick can also be considered for other methods. However, in the case of GD and Nesterov, it
493       does not alter the method since the data matrix A and the loss function ℓ are not split. All methods
494       start from zero.

1029   Here proxγf is a proximal operator (Parikh et al., 2014): proxγf (x) = arg miny∈Rd (γf (y) + 21 ∥x −
1030
       y∥2 ). In the general case, solving an additional minimization problem to calculate such an operator
1031   is necessary. But, in the case of simple, proximal-friendly functions ℓ and r, the proximal operator
1032   has a closed-form solution and can be computed exactly and sometimes for free. Theorem B.1 gives
1033   the convergence, and proof can be found in Appendix D.2.

1420   Before we start proving Theorem B.1, we need a small lemma concerning the proximal operator.
1421
       Lemma D.3. Let h be convex and z + = proxγh (z) with some γ > 0. Then for all x ∈ Rd the
1422   following inequality holds
1423
                                  ⟨z + − z, x − z + ⟩ ≥ γ h(z + ) − h(x) .

1431

4023   D.11    O N CONVERGENCE GAP
4024
4025   In our theoretical analysis, we use the criterion: gap(x, z, y) := maxỹ∈Y L(x, z, ỹ) −
4026   minx̃,z̃∈X ,Z L(x̃, z̃, y), where L(x, z, y) = ℓ(z, b) + r(x) + y T (Ax − z).      Since
4027   minx̃,z̃∈X ,Z L(x̃, z̃, y) ≤ L(x∗ , z ∗ , y), we get
4028
                                           max L(x, z, ỹ) − L(x∗ , z ∗ , y) ≤ gap(x, z, y).
                                            ỹ∈Y


                                                                       75
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


4029
       We note that Ax∗ = z ∗ , then
4030
4031   max L(x, z, ỹ) − L(x∗ , z ∗ , y) = [ℓ(z, b) + r(x) + max ỹ T (Ax − z)] − [ℓ(z ∗ , b) + r(x∗ ) + (y)T (Ax∗ − z ∗ )]
       ỹ∈Y                                                      ỹ∈Y
4032
4033                                     = [ℓ(z, b) + r(x) + max ỹ T (Ax − z)] − [ℓ(Ax∗ , b) + r(x∗ )].
                                                                 ỹ∈Y
4034
4035   When taking maximum for y ∈ Y we can define Y as we need. In particular, we can choose
4036
       Y = [y ∈ Rs | ||y||∞ ≤ C] for some C > 0. Then
4037                             max ỹ T (Ax − z) = C∥Ax − z k ∥1 ≥ C∥Ax − z∥.
4038                             ỹ∈Y

4039   Finally, we get
4040
4041          gap(x, z, y) ≥ [ℓ(z, b) + r(x) − ℓ(Ax∗ , b) + r(x∗ )] + C∥Axk − z k ∥ = newgap(x, z).
4042   If it holds that gap(x, z, y) ≤ ε, we guarantee that newgap(x, z, y) ≤ ε. The question that arises is
4043   whether newgap(x, z, y) ≤ ε implies that [ℓ(z, b) + r(x) − ℓ(Ax∗ , b) + r(x∗ )] as well as ∥Ax − z∥
4044   are also “small” in the sense that they are smaller than ε (up to constants). In general, the answer
4045   is no: [ℓ(z, b) + r(x) − ℓ(Ax∗ , b) + r(x∗ )] might be very small (and negative), and ∥Ax − z∥2
4046   can be very large. But Theorem 3.60 from (Beck, 2017) shows that if C is large enough such a
4047   conclusion can be drawn. In particular, if newgap(xk , z k , y k ) ≤ ε then C∥Axk − z k ∥2 ≤ ε and we
4048   have Axk → z k .

       E    A DDITIONAL E XPERIMENTS
4084
4085
       In the main part (Figure 1 of Section 6) we shown that the concept of the saddle point reformulation
4086
       and Algorithm 1 for its solution is competitive in the deterministic case. Here we present additional
4087   experiments.
4088
4089   As in the main part, we conduct experiments on the linear regression problem:
4090
       minx∈Rd f (x) = 21 ∥Ax − b∥2 + λ∥x∥22 . We take mushrooms, a9a, w8a and MNIST datasets
       from LibSVM library (Chang & Lin, 2011). We vertically (by features) uniformly divide the whole
4091
       dataset between 5 devices.
4092
4093   First we repeat the same experiments as in the main part, but now for each method we tune the
4094   parameters using a grid search. The results are shown in Figure 3. If we compare Figure 1 and
4095   Figure 3, the one method that accelerates the most is Algorithm 1.

4114
       Figure 3: Comparison of tuned methods for solving the VFL problem in different formulations: minimization
       (GD, Nesterov) and saddle point (ADMM, ExtraGradient/Algorithm 1). The comparison is made on
4115   LibSVM datasets mushrooms, a9a, w8a and MNIST.
4116
4117
       Next, we want to consider modifications of Algorithm 1 and show that they can speed Algorithm 1
4118
       up from different points of view.
4119
4120   In the first group of experiments with modifications (Figure 4), we test the performance of Algorithm
4121   2. We use the compression operator Q = RandK%, which is a random selection coordinates: 100%
4122
       (Algorithm 1), 50%, 25%, 10%. An important detail is that we set the same random generator
       and seed on each of the devices. Therefore, at each iteration we send random coordinates, but
4123
       they are the same for all devices. The comparison is made in terms of the number of full vectors
4124
       transmitted. In contrast to the main part, here we tune stepsizes, since with the theoretical step it is
4125   not possible to achieve the best acceleration compared to Algorithm 1. The comparison is done in
4126   two settings: the basic one and using the β-trick (see disscusion after Corollary 2.3). The results
4127   show that compression can indeed speed up the communication process.
4128
       In the second group of experiments with modifications (Figure 5), we test the performance of Al-
4129
       gorithm 3 in comparison with Algorithm 2. We use the compression operators C = TopK% (for
4130
       Algorithm 3), which is a greedy selection coordinates, and Q = RandK% (for Algorithm 2) with K
4131   = 25% and 10%. The comparison is made in terms of the number of full vectors transmitted. As
4132   in the previous experiment, we tune stepsizes. In experiments, we see that unbiased compression
4133   outperforms biased compression almost always. In the horizontal case, the opposite is usually true
4134   (Beznosikov et al., 2020). We attribute this effect to the fact that in the case of RandK% compres-
4135   sion we set the same random generator and seed on different devices and therefore they send the
4136   same random coordinates at each iteration. In the case of using TopK% operator we cannot do this,
       therefore convergence is worse.


                                                                                                               77
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


4137                                                                             MUSHROOMS (TUNED)                                                                                                 A9A (TUNED)

       Figure 4: Comparison of Algorithm 2 for solving the VFL problem (4). The comparison is made on LibSVM
4169
       datasets mushrooms, a9a, w8a and MNIST. The compression operator Q = RandK%. The criterion for
4170   comparison is the number of full vectors transmitted. The top line reflects the work of methods on the basic
4171   problem, the bottom line solves the problem with the β-trick (see disscusion after Corollary 2.3).
4172
4173   In the third group of experiments with modifications (Figure 6), we test the performance of Algo-
4174   rithm 4. At each iteration we generate only 2 devices out of 5 that communicate. The comparison is
4175
       made in terms of the number of full vectors transmitted from all devices. As in the previous experi-
       ments, we tune stepsizes. The results show that the partial participation technique can indeed speed
4176
       up the communication process in terms of the number of devices communicated.
4177
4178   In the fouth group of experiments with modifications (Figure 7), we test the performance of Al-
4179   gorithm 5. We use a random selection coordinates: 100% (Algorithm 1), 50%, 25%, 10%. An
4180   important detail is that we set the same random generator and seed on each of the devices. The
4181
       comparison is made in terms of the computational powers. Here we also tune stepsizes. The results
       show that the random coordinate selection can indeed speed up the computational process.
4182
4183
4184
       E.1   T ECHNICAL DETAILS
4185
       Our algorithms is written in Python 3.10, with the use of PyTorch optimization library. We imple-
4186   ment a simulation of distributed optimization system on a single server. Our server is AMD Ryzen
4187   Threadripper 2950X 16-Core Processor @ 2.2 GHz CPU and x2 NVIDIA GeForce GTX 1080 Ti
4188   GPU.
